The Maturidi Principle of Tafwid: Negating Resemblance and Affirming the Text
Maturidi lays down the principle of tafwid for divine attributes based on 'nafi al-shabah'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi approach to divine attributes: affirming the revealed text while negating any resemblance to creation, without specifying a particular interpretation.

Core Idea
Tafwid means affirming the text without explaining how.

Imam al-Maturidi establishes a foundational principle for dealing with divine attributes. Based on the Quranic verse 'Nothing is like Him,' he argues that we must affirm what revelation says—such as Allah's rising over the Throne—without assigning a specific interpretation. This approach, known as tafwid, protects divine transcendence while honoring the literal text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read Maturidi's statement and the translation. Notice how he begins with the Quranic verse as the foundation.
2
Identify the Quranic basis
Find the verse 'Nothing is like Him' and understand its role. Maturidi uses it to negate resemblance.
3
Trace the reasoning on 'istawa'
Maturidi says we must say 'the Most Merciful rose over the Throne' as revelation states, but we do not assign a specific interpretation due to multiple possibilities.
4
Apply to other attributes
The same principle applies to all attributes like 'ru'ya' (vision of Allah). Negate resemblance, affirm the text, believe in God's intent.
This principle is called tafwid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وأما الأصل عندنا في ذلك أن الله تعالى قال: ﴿لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ﴾ فنفى عن نفسه شبه خلقه، وقد بينا أنه في فعله وصفته متعال عن الأشباه، فيجب القول بالرحمن على العرش استوى على ما جاء به التنزيل، وثبت ذلك في العقل، ثم لا نقطع تأويله على شيء لاحتماله غيره مما ذكرنا، واحتماله أيضا ما لم يبلغنا مما يعلم أنه غير محتمل شبه الخلق، ونؤمن بما أراد الله به، وكذلك في كل أمر ثبت التنزيل فيه نحو الرؤية وغير ذلك، يجب نفي الشبه عنه والإيمان بما أراده من غير تحقيق على شيء دون شيء. والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: As for the principle with us in that matter, it is that Allah said: 'Nothing is like Him' – so He negated from Himself resemblance to His creation. And we have explained that He, in His action and attribute, is exalted above likenesses. So it is necessary to say that the Most Merciful rose over the Throne according to what the revelation brought, and that is established in the intellect. Then we do not definitively assign its interpretation to something specific, due to its possibility of meaning other than what we mentioned, and also its possibility of what has not reached us, which is known to be not resembling the creation. And we believe in what Allah intended by it. And similarly in every matter where the revelation is established, such as the vision (al-ru'ya) and other than that, it is necessary to negate resemblance from Him and to believe in what He intended, without specifying a particular interpretation. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage is a statement of the foundational Maturidi principle regarding divine attributes. Maturidi begins with the Quranic verse 'Nothing is like Him' as the basis: God negates any likeness to His creation. Since God is exalted above all resemblances in both action and attribute, we must affirm what the revelation says—specifically, that 'the Most Merciful rose over the Throne'—without determining a particular interpretation (ta'wil) because the verse could bear multiple meanings that are all free from anthropomorphism. We do not commit to one interpretation but believe in whatever God intended. This same approach applies to all revealed attributes, such as the vision of God (ru'ya): we negate resemblance and believe without specifying how. The key is 'tafwid'—consigning the precise meaning to God while affirming the literal text without corrupting it with human concepts.

Key Terms
ليس كمثله شيء
تشبيه
تنزيل
تأويل
رؤية
تفويض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think tafwid means denying the attribute or negating its reality altogether.
Correction: Maturidi shows that tafwid does not deny the attribute; rather, it affirms the text as revealed while negating any resemblance to creation and consigning the precise modality to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what should one do regarding the interpretation of 'istawa' and similar attributes?
A) Interpret them in a way that fits human understanding
B) Negate their literal meaning entirely
C) Affirm them as revealed without specifying a meaning
D) Allegorize them to avoid any implication

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's principle of tafwid requires affirming the revealed text about Allah's attributes without specifying a particular interpretation, trusting that Allah's intended meaning is free from anthropomorphism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about Allah's attributes (like His rising over the Throne), do you find yourself tempted to imagine a physical form or movement? How does Maturidi's principle of negating resemblance while affirming the text challenge your natural inclination to visualize? Commit to reciting 'Nothing is like Him' whenever such thoughts arise.
?
The principle of tafwid requires you to entrust the true meaning of Allah's attributes to Him without demanding an explanation. Is there an attribute of Allah that you struggle to accept without understanding the 'how'? Why does your heart resist surrendering to Allah's transcendence in this area? What would it look like to truly believe in what Allah intended without needing to know the modality?
?
Maturidi says we must believe in the vision of Allah (ru'ya) without specifying how. Do you ever doubt such promises because you cannot conceive of them? How does the call to tafwid free you from the anxiety of having to explain the unseen? Will you trust Allah's promise even when your mind cannot picture it?

The Epistemological Basis for Tafwid
Maturidi explains why human concepts cannot be applied to divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that prior knowledge of God's uniqueness prevents interpreting divine attributes using human concepts.

Core Idea
God's transcendence blocks analogy: we cannot apply human meanings to His attributes.

Maturidi presents a crucial epistemological principle: before hearing any attribute verse, we already know that Allah is absolutely unlike creation. This prior knowledge constrains how we understand those verses. In this passage, he explains why interpreting divine attributes based on human concepts is impermissible and why a stance of tafwid (pausing at the literal text) is required.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the English translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the dense, philosophical style. Maturidi is building a logical case.
2
Identify the key premise
The passage begins by stating that Allah is exalted above likenesses in essence and action. This is the foundation of the argument.
This premise is non-negotiable in Islamic theology.
3
Follow the logical move
Because Allah is unlike creation, we cannot transfer the meanings we derive from created things to Him. The text says 'it is not permissible to understand from the attribution to Him what is understood from other than Him.'
This is a direct refutation of anthropomorphism (tashbih).
4
Understand the role of prior knowledge
Maturidi emphasizes that Allah is known (by reason and revelation) to be different before we even hear a particular attribute verse. This prior knowledge prevents us from interpreting the verse based on human concepts.
This is the epistemic justification for tafwid.
5
Recognize the practical outcome
Since words like 'over', 'Throne', and 'rising' have multiple meanings in the witnessed world, we must choose the best (non-anthropomorphic) meaning or pause where the meaning is unknown. The test from Allah is to pause on certain matters.
Tafwid does not mean total ignorance but accepting the text without assigning a specific modality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل في هذا أن الأمر يضيق على السامع بما يقدره من المفهوم عن الخلق في الوجود، وإذ لزم القول في الله بالتعالي عن الأشباه ذاتا وفعلا، لم يجز أن يفهم من الإضافة إليه المفهوم من غيره في الوجود، مع ما كان الوقوف على ما هو عليه على احتمال المعنى يصرف إليه الكلام في الخلق بما به من قبل سمع ذلك الكلام، والله سبحانه عرف قبل سمع ذلك الكلام على غير الذي عرف عليه الخلق، لم يجز صرف التأويل إلى ما فهمه من الخلق: إذ سبق العلم المتقدم منه، ذلك المعنى معنى قد يفهم من الشاهد من على، ومن العرش، ومن الاستواء معان مختلفة، لم يجز صرف ذلك إلى أوحش وجه، وثمة لأحسن ذلك مساغ ما كان الله يمتحن بالوقوف في أشياء كما جاء من نعوت الوعد والوعيد، وما [؟].
The principle in this is that the matter becomes restricted upon the listener by what he estimates from the understanding of creation in existence. And when the statement regarding Allah is necessitated by His being exalted above likenesses in essence and action, it is not permissible to understand from the attribution to Him what is understood from other than Him in existence, given the [primal] pause on [the fact] that [the meaning] is as it is, upon the possibility of the meaning to which the speech is directed among creation, based on what it had before hearing that speech. And Allah is known before hearing that speech in a way different from that which creation is known. So it is not permissible to direct the interpretation to what one understood from creation, because there is precedence of knowledge from Him. That meaning – a meaning that is understood from the witnessed world – regarding [terms like] 'over' and 'the Throne' and 'rising' have different meanings, so it is not permissible to direct that to the ugliest aspect, and there is room for the best of that as long as Allah tests [people] by pausing on matters such as the attributes of promise and threat and what [؟].
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological argument for tafwid. Maturidi begins by stating that the issue becomes difficult for the listener because he naturally tries to understand divine attributes based on his experience of created things. However, since we know that Allah is absolutely beyond any resemblance to creation in essence and action, we cannot apply the meanings understood from creation to Him. Before hearing the Quranic attribute, Allah is already known to be unlike anything. Therefore, we cannot interpret the attribute based on created concepts because we already have prior knowledge that He is different. The terms 'over,' 'Throne,' and 'rising' have multiple meanings in human language, but we must not choose the ugliest (i.e., anthropomorphic) meaning; rather, we must assume the best possible meaning while acknowledging that Allah tests people by requiring them to pause (stop at the literal text without interpretation) in matters like promises and threats.

Key Terms
تعالي
مفهوم
إضافة
تأويل
شاهد
وقوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think we can understand God's attributes by analogy with creatures.
Correction: Maturidi says prior knowledge of God's uniqueness prevents that analogy.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say it is not permissible to interpret divine attributes based on human understanding?
A) Because human language is inadequate
B) Because God is known to be unlike creation before hearing the attribute
C) Because the Quran does not specify meanings
D) Because the companions disagreed on interpretation

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that because we already know Allah is unlike anything, we cannot understand His attributes using human concepts; instead, we must pause and accept the text without anthropomorphic interpretations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah's attributes in human terms—like picturing Him sitting on a throne or having hands? What does that reveal about your heart's inclination to reduce the transcendent to the familiar? Will you now consciously stop and say 'I do not know how, but I believe as He described without likeness'?
?
When you read verses like 'The Most Merciful rose over the Throne', do you feel a tension between what the words suggest to your mind and what your faith tells you about Allah's uniqueness? Are you willing to leave that tension unresolved, trusting that Allah knows best, rather than forcing an interpretation?
?
You have known since childhood that Allah is unlike anything—but does that knowledge actually govern how you approach every ambiguous verse? Or do you unconsciously let your imagination run? From this moment, will you commit to pausing (wuqūf) and saying 'Allah knows best' every time you encounter such attributes?

Affirming Divine Attributes without Ta'wil
Maturidi establishes the principle of tafwid for divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi principle of affirming divine attributes as revealed while consigning their exact interpretation to Allah.

Core Idea
Affirm the wording, negate similarity, consign the meaning.

Maturidi lays down the foundational methodology for dealing with divine attributes in revelation. He cites the verse 'There is nothing like Him' to establish that Allah is absolutely unlike creation. From this, he derives that we must affirm attributes like istiwā' without determining a particular interpretation, because multiple meanings are possible as long as they avoid anthropomorphism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the structure: a foundational verse, an obligation, a prohibition, and a general rule.
Pay attention to the phrase 'then we do not determine its interpretation'.
2
Identify the foundational verse
Locate the verse 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11) in the passage. Understand how Maturidi uses it to negate any similarity between Allah and creation.
This verse is the cornerstone of the principle.
3
Understand the principle of tafwid
Maturidi says we must affirm what revelation says about attributes, but we do not fix a single interpretation. Instead, we believe in whatever Allah intended, knowing it is free from anthropomorphism. Recognize that this applies to all revealed attributes like ru'ya.
Tafwid means consigning the meaning to Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : وأما الأصل عندنا في ذلك أن الله تعالى قال : لَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ فنفى عن نفسه شبه خلقه، وقد بينا أنه في فعله وصفته متعال عن الأشباه، فيجب القول بالرحمن على العرش استوى على ما جاء به التنزيل، وثبت ذلك في العقل، ثم لا نقطع تأويله على شيء لاحتماله غيره مما ذكرنا، واحتماله أيضا ما لم يبلغنا مما يعلم أنه غير محتمل شبه الخلق، ونؤمن بما أراد الله به، وكذلك في كل أمر ثبت التنزيل فيه نحو الرؤية وغير ذلك، يجب نفى الشبه عنه والإيمان بما أراده من غير تحقيق على شيء دون شيء، والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: As for the principle with us in that: Allah said: 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11), so He negated from Himself similarity to His creation, and we have shown that He in His action and attribute is exalted above similarities. So it is necessary to say about 'the Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne' as the revelation came, and that is established in the intellect. Then we do not determine its interpretation to be one thing because of its possibility of other than what we mentioned, and also its possibility of what has not reached us, knowing that it is not likely to be similar to creation. And we believe in what Allah intended by it. Likewise in every matter in which revelation is established, such as vision and others, it is necessary to negate similarity from Him and to believe in what He intended, without specifying one thing rather than another. And Allah is the Granter of success.
Source pages p. 673

Commentary
This passage establishes the Ash'ari-Maturidi principle of tafwid (consigning the meaning to Allah) with respect to divine attributes. Maturidi begins by citing the Qur'anic verse 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11) as the foundational proof that Allah is absolutely unlike His creation. He then states that it is obligatory to affirm what the revelation has affirmed, such as 'the Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne' (istiwā'), and that this is rationally established. However, he insists that we must not fix a particular interpretation (ta'wil) for such verses, because they can bear multiple meanings, including meanings that may not have reached us but are nonetheless free from any similarity to creation. The correct stance is to believe in whatever Allah intended without assigning a definite interpretation. This principle applies to all revealed attributes, such as the vision of Allah in the afterlife (ru'ya). The key is to negate any anthropomorphic similarity while affirming the reality of the attribute as revealed.

Key Terms
أصل
التنزيل
التأويل
نفي الشبه
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that affirming the literal wording of attributes like 'istiwā' implies anthropomorphism or that we must interpret them metaphorically to avoid it.
Correction: Maturidi teaches that we affirm the wording as revealed, but we negate any similarity to creation and consign the exact interpretation to Allah (tafwid). This avoids both anthropomorphism and distortion.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the correct stance regarding attributes like istiwā' mentioned in revelation?
A) They must be interpreted metaphorically to avoid anthropomorphism.
B) They are affirmed as revealed, but their exact interpretation is not specified.
C) They are to be rejected because they imply similarity to creation.
D) They are to be understood literally without any qualification.

Key Takeaway
The correct approach to divine attributes is to affirm them as revealed, negate any similarity to creation, and consign the exact meaning to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite a verse like 'The Most Merciful established Himself above the Throne,' does your mind instinctively try to picture something? How does Maturidi's instruction to not fix a single interpretation challenge you to submit to Allah's knowledge rather than your imagination? What step will you take this week to guard your heart against picturing Allah?
?
You have just learned that the correct stance is to believe in what Allah intended without specifying. Is there an attribute of Allah that you have secretly tried to 'figure out' or explain away? Are you willing to leave that mystery with Allah and simply affirm the wording? What would that surrender cost you today?
?
The lesson anchors everything in the verse 'There is nothing like Him.' Do you truly believe that nothing—no image, no concept, no analogy—can represent Allah? How does that reshape the way you speak about Allah in your daily conversations? Will you catch yourself next time you are tempted to use a comparison?

The Various Meanings of God's Nearness (Qurb)
Maturidi enumerates four meanings of divine nearness with scriptural proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's nearness in the Quran and Hadith is not spatial but encompasses relational meanings such as response, support, rank, and protection.

Core Idea
God's nearness is not spatial but multiple spiritual relations.

Maturidi, after establishing God's transcendence, now classifies the different meanings of 'nearness' (qurb) as used in revelation. This passage is crucial because it prevents a literal, anthropomorphic understanding of God being 'close'. By examining each scriptural example, we learn that divine nearness encompasses response, support, rank, and protection—all without spatial limitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it, then read the full literal translation. Let the author's rhythm come through before interpreting.
The passage has some OCR issues, but the meaning is clear.
2
Identify the four types of nearness
Maturidi lists four distinct meanings: nearness via response, support, drawing near in rank, and protection. Locate each one in the text and note the evidence cited.
3
Examine the scriptural proofs
For each type, reflect on the Quranic verse or hadith. Consider how each proof illustrates a different facet of divine nearness.
Pay attention to the hadith about drawing near by a handspan.
4
Connect to the overall lesson
Ask yourself why Maturidi felt the need to clarify these meanings. How does this classification safeguard tawhid?
This guards against the misconception of spatial nearness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا فيما تقدم وصف قربه، وذلك بالإجابة كقوله تعالى : وَإِذَا سَأَلَكَ عادِي عَنِّي فَإِنِّي قَرِيب ، وبالنصر والمعونه كقوله تعالى: إِنَّ اللَّهَ مَعَ الَّذِينَ انه وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ مُحْسِنُونَ ٣٤ ، وبالتقرب إلى المنزلة والمحل كقوله تعالى : واسْجُدْ .. اقترب ، وما روى : أن من تقرب إلى شبرا تقربت إليه ذراعا ، إلى آخر ذلك ، وقوله : وَابْتَغُوا إِلَيْهِ الوسيلة ٦٠ ، وفى الكلاة والحفظ كقوله : وَرَبُّكَ عَلى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ حفيظ ، وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيء وكيل .
And we have explained previously the description of His nearness. That is: through response, as His saying exalted: 'And when My servants ask you concerning Me, indeed I am near' (2:186); and through support and help, as His saying exalted: 'Indeed Allah is with those who fear Him and those who are doers of good' (16:128); and through drawing near in rank and station, as His saying exalted: 'Prostrate and draw near' (96:19) and what is narrated: 'Whoever draws near to Me by a handspan, I draw near to him by a cubit,' to the end of that; and His saying: 'And seek the means of approach to Him' (5:35); and in protection and preservation, as His saying: 'And your Lord is over all things a Guardian' (34:21) and 'He is over all things a Disposer' (39:62).
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the meanings of God's nearness (qurb). Maturidi states that he has already described the nature of God's nearness. He then enumerates four types with scriptural evidence: (1) Nearness through answering prayers (Qur'an 2:186). (2) Nearness through support and help (Qur'an 16:128, with variant reading). (3) Nearness in rank and station through acts of worship, citing Qur'an 96:19 and the hadith of drawing near by handspan. (4) Nearness in protection and preservation (Qur'an 34:21, 39:62). Each type is distinct from spatial closeness, affirming God's transcendence.

المعونة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's nearness (qurb) is literal spatial closeness to creatures.
Correction: God is transcendent, and 'nearness' in revelation refers to relational meanings such as answering prayer, supporting the righteous, elevating rank, and protecting all things.

Checkpoint

A) Spatial proximity to all things.
B) Nearness through response to supplication.
C) Nearness by being physically inside creation.
D) Nearness through incarnation.

Key Takeaway
God's nearness is a multifaceted concept that must be understood through the lens of revelation, not physical proximity.

?

The hadith says that if you draw near to Allah by a handspan, He draws near to you by a cubit. Think of a habit or sin you have been holding onto, knowing it distances you. What is the one 'handspan' move you have been avoiding? Why, and what is stopping you from making it today?

If God's nearness through 'support and help' (16:128) is conditional on taqwa and ihsan, examine your own heart: in which area of your life are you failing to fear Allah or to do good? Be specific. The True Meaning of God's Nearness
Maturidi categorizes the various meanings of God's nearness in scripture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple ways the Qur'an describes God's nearness, all of which are metaphorical and free from spatial or physical connotations.

Core Idea
God's nearness is not spatial but relational—through response, support, rank, and knowledge.

Maturidi begins by unpacking the verses that speak of God's nearness. He shows that nearness in the Qur'an takes various forms: divine response to supplication, divine support of the righteous, elevation in rank through worship, and God's encompassing knowledge and guardianship. This classification helps us avoid literal interpretations that would imply physical proximity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on nearness
Read the Arabic and translation focusing on the list of verses Maturidi cites. Notice that Maturidi does not give a single definition but rather a range of meanings derived from different verses.
The verse 'Prostrate and draw near' shows that nearness can be achieved through worship.
2
Identify the categories of nearness
List the types Maturidi mentions: response, support, rank, guardianship, knowledge, etc. See that all these are relational, not spatial.
3
Contrast with false understanding
Understand why God cannot be literally near in space. Maturidi implies that true nearness is about relationship, not location.
This sets the stage for later discussion of movement terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد بينا فيما تقدم وصف قربه، وذلك بالإجابة كقوله تعالى: "وَإِذَا سَأَلَكَ عِبَادِي عَنِّي فَإِنِّي قَرِيبٌ"، وبالنصر والمعونة كقوله تعالى: "إِنَّ اللَّهَ مَعَ الَّذِينَ اتَّقَوْا وَالَّذِينَ هُمْ مُحْسِنُونَ"، وبالتقرب إلى المنزلة والمحل كقوله تعالى: "وَاسْجُدْ وَاقْتَرِبْ"، وما روي أن من تقرب إلى شبرا تقربت إليه ذراعا، إلى آخر ذلك، وقوله: "وَابْتَغُوا إِلَيْهِ الْوَسِيلَةَ"، وفي الكلاة والحفظ كقوله: "وَرَبُّكَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ حَفِيظٌ"، "وَهُوَ عَلَى كُلِّ شَيْءٍ وَكِيلٌ"، وقوله: "أَفَمَنْ هُوَ قَائِمٌ عَلَى كُلِّ نَفْسٍ بِمَا كَسَبَتْ"، وبالعلم بقوله: "يَعْلَمُ سِرَّكُمْ وَجَهْرَكُمْ"، وغير ذلك. فعلى مثل بعض هذه الوجوه المجيء والذهاب والقعود مع ما كان مجنى الأجسام يفهم منه الانتقال، ثم مجيء الحق يفهم منه الظهور كقوله: "قُلْ جَاءَ الْحَقُّ"، وعلى ذلك ذهاب الباطل بطلانه، وذهاب الجسم انتقاله، فهذا محل المجيء والذهاب في المعروف من الأعراض والأجسام، والله يتعالى عن المعنيين جميعاً، لم يجز أن يفهم من المضاف إليه ذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله. ثم للمسألة عبارة أخرى: إنه ما من جهة ولا حالة إلا لله على عباده فيها نعم لا تحصى، فجعيل عليهم بنها، وفيها عبادات، كما جعل في الجوارح والأموال بها له فيهما من النعم، ولا قوة إلا بالله. على أن السماء هي محل ومهبط الوحي، ومنها أصول بركات الدنيا، فرفع إليها البصر لذلك، ولا قوة إلا بالله.
We have already explained the description of His nearness, which is through response, as His saying: "And when My servants ask you about Me, indeed I am near." And through support and assistance, as His saying: "Indeed Allah is with those who fear Him and those who are doers of good." And through nearness in rank and station, as His saying: "Prostrate and draw near." And what is narrated: "Whoever draws near to Me by a span, I draw near to him by a cubit," etc. And His saying: "Seek means of nearness to Him." And in guardianship and preservation, as His saying: "And your Lord is over all things a Guardian," and "He is over all things a Disposer." And His saying: "Is He who stands over every soul with what it has earned?" And through knowledge, as His saying: "He knows your secret and your public." And other [verses]. So according to some of these meanings, [the terms] coming, going, and sitting – along with what is attributed to bodies, from which movement is understood – [are interpreted differently]. Then the coming of truth is understood as manifestation, as His saying: "Say, truth has come." And similarly, the going of falsehood is its vanishing, while the going of a body is its movement. This is the meaning of coming and going in what is known of accidents and bodies. And Allah is exalted above both meanings. So it is not permissible to understand from what is attributed to Him [literal] movement. And there is no power except with Allah. Then, there is another expression for this issue: There is no direction or state except that Allah has blessings upon His servants in it that cannot be counted, so He made upon them therein acts of worship, just as He made in the limbs and wealth blessings for them in them. And there is no power except with Allah. And because the sky is the place of revelation and the source of the blessings of this world, the gaze is raised to it for that reason. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 675

Commentary
This passage classifies the types of God's nearness mentioned in revelation. Maturidi first lists several forms: nearness through answering prayer (response), through support, through rank (as in prostration), through seeking means, through guardianship, knowledge, etc. He then addresses verses that describe God as 'coming' or 'going'. He argues that these terms, when applied to God, do not imply physical movement. For example, 'the coming of truth' means its manifestation, and 'the going of falsehood' means its annihilation. Since God transcends the properties of bodies and accidents, such terms must be understood metaphorically. He then adds a note about the sky: the sky is the place where revelation descends and from which worldly blessings come, so raising the eyes to it is appropriate, not because God is there, but because it is the source of His bounties.

Key Terms
قرب
الإجابة
النصر
التقرب
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's nearness means He is physically close in space.
Correction: Maturidi shows that nearness is always relational—through response, support, rank, guardianship, or knowledge—never spatial or physical.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the verse 'Prostrate and draw near' (96:19) indicate about God's nearness?
A) Physical proximity
B) Nearness in rank through worship
C) Nearness in time
D) Nearness in space

Key Takeaway
Maturidi classifies God's nearness into multiple relational categories, none of which imply physical proximity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these six categories of nearness do you most often neglect in your daily life? What specific action can you take today to live in that neglected dimension of closeness to God?
?
When you read that God is 'with those who fear Him,' does that comfort you or convict you? What would change if you truly believed He is your present support in your weakest moment right now?
?
You recite verses about God's nearness—but do you actually seek Him through the means He prescribed, or do you expect closeness without the effort of 'wasila'? Name one 'wasila' you will commit to this week.

The Metaphorical Interpretation of Coming and Going
Maturidi explains the metaphorical meaning of 'coming' and 'going' in revelation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic terms 'coming' and 'going' when attributed to God must be interpreted metaphorically as manifestation and annihilation, not as physical movement.

Core Idea
God's 'coming' means manifestation of truth; His 'going' means annihilation of falsehood.

Having established that God's nearness is not spatial, Maturidi now tackles the more challenging terms 'coming' and 'going' which appear in verses. He argues that these too are metaphorical: the 'coming of truth' is its manifestation, and the 'going of falsehood' is its obliteration. Since God transcends the properties of bodies, assigning literal movement to Him is impossible. He then adds a final note about why the sky is considered the direction of prayer.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on coming and going
Read the Arabic and translation from 'فعلى مثل بعض هذه الوجوه...' to '...ولا قوة إلا بالله.' Notice how Maturidi distinguishes between the literal movement of bodies and the metaphorical use in scripture.
'Majā' al-ḥaqq' is a well-known Qur'anic phrase.
2
Understand the key analogy
Compare the 'coming of truth' to manifestation and the 'going of falsehood' to annihilation. Maturidi explicitly parallels these: for bodies, coming and going imply movement; for abstract concepts like truth and falsehood, they imply appearance and disappearance.
3
Grasp why literal interpretation is forbidden
Maturidi states that Allah is exalted above the meanings of accidents and bodies. This is a fundamental principle of tanzih (divine transcendence). Any term implying physicality must be reinterpreted.
4
Reflect on the sky note
Read the final part about the sky. Maturidi notes that the sky is the source of revelation and blessings, so raising eyes to it is appropriate, not because Allah is there.
This clarifies why prayer is directed towards the sky without implying God's spatial location.

The Ruling on Seeing Allah and the Proof from Quran 6:103
Maturidi states the ruling on ru'ya and gives a proof from Quran 6:103.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maturidi position on seeing Allah in the afterlife and how the verse 'Sights do not comprehend Him' actually supports its possibility.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is affirmed without modality; the verse negates comprehension, not sight.

Maturidi opens this foundational topic by stating the Ahl al-Sunnah position on ru'ya: it is necessary and true, but without how. He then presents the first proof, drawing on a key Quranic verse that at first glance might seem to deny vision. In this lesson, we will examine his argument and see why the negation of idrak (comprehension) actually implies the reality of ru'ya.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words sink in before interpreting.
2
Identify the ruling on ru'ya
Notice that Maturidi says ru'ya is 'lazim' (necessary) and 'haqq' (true), but he immediately adds 'without idrak and without tafsir.' That means we affirm the vision but do not know its modality.
This is the standard Ahl al-Sunnah position.
3
Understand the proof logic
The verse says 'Sights do not comprehend Him.' Maturidi argues: if seeing were impossible, negating comprehension would be pointless. The negation only makes sense if seeing is possible but comprehension is not.
He contrasts with created things that can be comprehended without being seen (e.g., through other senses).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The conclusion is that ru'ya is affirmed, but idrak (encompassing, full comprehension) is negated. This preserves both the reality of the vision and the transcendence of Allah.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you can explain the proof in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مسألة رؤية الله قال أبو منصور رحمة الله : القول في رؤية الرب عز وجل عندنا لازم وحق من غير إدراك ولا تفسير. فأما الدليل على الرؤية فقوله تعالى: لَا تُدْرِكُهُ الأَبْصَارُ وَهُوَ يُدْرِكُ الأَبْصَارَ ، ولو كان لا يرى لم يكن لنفي الإدراك حكمة؛ إذ يدرك غيره بغير رؤية ، فموضع نفى الإدراك - وغيره من الخلق لا يدرك إلا بالرؤية . لا معنى له ، وبالله التوفيق.
The question of seeing Allah. Abu Mansur, may Allah have mercy on him, said: The statement concerning seeing the Lord, mighty and majestic, is necessary and true for us, without perception (idrak) and without interpretation (tafsir). As for the proof for seeing: His saying, exalted: 'Sights do not comprehend Him, and He comprehends sights.' And if He were not seen, then the negation of comprehension would have no wisdom; for others are comprehended without seeing. So the position of negating comprehension — and others among creation are not comprehended except by seeing — has no meaning. And success is from Allah.
Source pages p. 676

Commentary
This passage defines the Maturidi position on the vision of Allah (ru'ya) and provides the first proof. The author, Abu Mansur al-Maturidi, begins by stating that affirming the vision of Allah is necessary (lazim) and true (haqq) for us, but without modality (idrak) or interpretation (tafsir). This means we believe it will happen in the afterlife but we do not know how. Then he gives a proof from the Quran, verse 6:103: 'Sights do not comprehend Him, and He comprehends sights.' He argues that if Allah were not seeable, then the negation of comprehension (idrak) by eyesight would be pointless, because other things that are not seen can be comprehended by other means. The point is that the verse negates comprehension, not vision itself. Therefore, vision is possible, and the negation of comprehension is meaningful only because vision occurs but without full comprehension.

Key Terms
رؤية
إدراك
الأبصار
لازم
حق
تفسير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many assume that 'sights do not comprehend Him' means Allah cannot be seen at all.
Correction: Maturidi argues the opposite: the verse negates comprehension (idrak), not vision (ru'ya). If vision were impossible, negating comprehension would be meaningless. The negation only makes sense if vision is possible but comprehension is not.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does the verse 'Sights do not comprehend Him' actually support the possibility of seeing Allah?
A) Because it proves that Allah is invisible.
B) Because it negates only comprehension, not sight itself, and if sight were impossible the negation would be meaningless.
C) Because it shows that prophets cannot see Allah.
D) Because it refers to the world, not the afterlife.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi affirms the vision of Allah in the afterlife, without modality, based on the Quranic negation of comprehension implying the reality of sight.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe you will see Allah in the Hereafter. Does this hope truly shape how you live today, or is it a distant fact that doesn’t move you? What one habit this week could you change to make that vision more real to you?
?
The author says ru'ya is 'necessary and true for us'. Do you actually feel its necessity in your heart, or have you merely accepted it as a creedal point? What would change if you truly lived as though you will see Him?
?
If you are honest, do you secretly harbor a fear that Allah is too distant to be seen, or that your sins have disqualified you? How does this lesson’s insistence that sight is possible without comprehension reshape your trust in His mercy?

The Proof from Musa's Request for Vision
Maturidi presents the proof from Musa's request and supporting points.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Prophet Musa's request to see Allah as a proof that seeing Allah is possible.

Core Idea
A prophet cannot ask for something rationally impossible.

Maturidi presents the second proof from the story of Musa. If seeing Allah were impossible, then Musa's request would be ignorance, but prophets are immune to ignorance of their Lord. Therefore, seeing Allah must be possible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get the overall argument.
Notice the logical structure: if P then Q, but not Q, therefore not P.
2
Understand the presumption
Why does Maturidi say that asking for the impossible would be ignorance? Because a rational person would not ask for something known to be impossible.
This is a basic rational principle.
3
Connect to prophethood
If Musa were ignorant of his Lord, he could not be a trustee of revelation. Since he is a prophet, the possibility of seeing Allah is affirmed.
Maturidi also notes that Allah did not forbid Musa, unlike other prophets.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : قول موسى عليه السلام : رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنْظُرُ إِلَيْكَ، ولو كان لا يجوز الرؤية لكان ذلك السؤال منه جهل بربه ، ومن يجهله لا يحتمل أن يكون موضعا لرسالته أمينا على وحيه . وبعاد ، فإن الله تعالى لم ينهه ولا أيأسه ، وبدون ذلك نهى نوحا وعاتب آدم وغيرهما من الرسل ، وذلك لو كان لا يجوز يبلغ الكفر . ثم قال : فإِنْ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَانِي ، فإن قيل : لعله سأل آية يعلم بها ، قيل : لا يحتمل ذا ارجوه : أحدها أنه قال : لَنْ تَرَانِي "، وقد أراه . وأيضا إن طلب الآيات يخرج مخرج التعنت ، أو قد أراه الآيات ، وذلك تعنت الكثرة ، انهم لا يزالون يطلبون الآيات وإن كانت الكفاية قد ثبتت فمثله ذلك . وأيضا أنه قال : " فإن استقر مكانه فسوف ترانى، ، والآية التي يستقر معها الجبل دون الآية التي لا يستقر معها . ثبت أنه لم يرد بذلك الآية ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the second: the saying of Musa, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me, that I may look at You.' And if seeing were not permissible, then that question from him would be ignorance of his Lord. And one who is ignorant of Him cannot be a place for His message, trusted with His revelation. And besides, Allah, exalted, did not forbid him nor despair him, and without that He forbade Nuh and rebuked Adam and others of the messengers. And that, if it were not permissible, would reach disbelief. Then He said: 'If it settles in its place, then you will see Me.' Then if it is said: perhaps he asked for a sign by which to know, it is said: that is not possible, the first of it: that He said: 'You will not see Me,' and He showed him. And also, asking for signs comes out as obstinacy, or He already showed him signs. And that is the obstinacy of many, they do not cease asking for signs even though sufficiency has been established, so such is that. And also that He said: 'If it settles in its place, then you will see Me,' and the sign for which the mountain settles is different from the sign for which it does not settle. So it is established that He did not intend by that a sign. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, from the story of Prophet Musa. Maturidi argues that if seeing Allah were impossible, then Prophet Musa's request to see Allah in this world would have been an act of ignorance about his Lord. But Musa was a prophet and messenger, so such ignorance cannot be attributed to him. Moreover, Allah did not forbid Musa from asking nor did He give him despair; whereas other prophets were rebuked for lesser matters. Then the verse says 'If it settles in its place, then you will see Me,' implying a conditional possibility. Then Maturidi addresses an objection: perhaps Musa asked for a sign, not actual seeing. He gives three arguments: 1) Allah already told Musa 'You will not see Me' and showed him something, so the request was not for a sign. 2) Asking for signs can be obstinate, especially after many signs. 3) The condition is about the mountain's stability, which is a sign, so the response is about seeing, not a sign. Therefore, the request was for actual vision.

Key Terms
موسى
رؤية
جهل
رسالة
أمانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would be the consequence if seeing Allah were impossible?
A) Musa's request would be an act of worship.
B) Musa's request would be ignorance of his Lord.
C) Musa would still be a prophet.
D) Allah would have forbidden him.

Key Takeaway
Musa's request to see Allah proves that seeing Allah is rationally possible, because a prophet cannot ask for the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you ask Allah for something in your du'a, do you truly believe He can grant it, or do you secretly limit His power? What would you ask for today if you were absolutely certain that nothing is impossible for Him?
?
Musa's request shows that he knew Allah is capable of showing Himself. Are there areas of your life where you act as if Allah is limited? What would change if you approached Him with the same boldness and trust as Musa?
?
This lesson teaches that a prophet cannot be ignorant of his Lord. You are not a prophet, but you are called to know Allah. In what ways have you settled for ignorance about His attributes, and what step will you take today to replace that ignorance with knowledge?

Refuting the Objection: Was Musa Asking for a Sign?
Maturidi refutes the objection that Musa asked for a sign.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's three arguments against the claim that Musa asked for a sign rather than actual vision.

Core Idea
Musa's request was for vision, not a sign, because he was already shown signs.

Some object that Musa's request was for a sign, not actual seeing. Maturidi refutes this with three arguments: Allah said 'You will not see Me' and then showed him something, asking for signs can be obstinate, and the condition of the mountain's stability indicates the request was about vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and refutation
Read the passage where Maturidi presents the objection and his three responses.
Identify each argument sequentially.
2
First argument: Allah said 'You will not see Me'
Maturidi notes that Allah already told Musa 'You will not see Me' and then showed him something, so the request was for vision itself.
3
Second argument: Asking for signs as obstinacy
Asking for signs after already seeing many can be obstinate, so Musa would not do that.
This aligns with how rejecters of truth ask for signs.
4
Third argument: The condition of the mountain
The sign of the mountain's stability is different from the sign that would be asked; thus the request was for actual vision.
The conditional 'if it settles' is about seeing, not about a sign.

Source Text

And also that He said: 'If it settles in its place, then you will see Me,' and the sign for which the mountain settles is different from the sign for which it does not settle. So it is established that He did not intend by that a sign. And there is no power except with Allah.

Ibrahim's Argument: Against Setting, Not Visibility
Maturidi analyzes Ibrahim's argument against celestial bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi uses Ibrahim's argument to support the possibility of seeing Allah, distinguishing between arguing against visibility versus arguing against impermanence.

Core Idea
Ibrahim argued against setting, not seeing — showing permanence, not invisibility, is key to divinity.

Maturidi draws on the Qur'anic story of Prophet Ibrahim, who reasoned against his people's worship of celestial bodies. Ibrahim did not say that a deity cannot be seen; he said a deity that sets cannot be divine. This distinction is crucial for Maturidi's argument that seeing Allah is possible because Allah is permanent and does not set.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to understand Maturidi's concise point.
Notice the contrast between 'seen' and 'sets'.
2
Understand Ibrahim's unique argument
Identify why Ibrahim focused on setting rather than visibility. This is key to Maturidi's proof.
3
Connect to the possibility of seeing Allah
Reflect on how not setting (permanence) makes seeing possible, while setting (impermanence) disqualifies divinity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا محاجة ابراهيم قومه في النجوم ، وما ذكر بالأفول والغيبة ، ولم يحاجهم بأن لا يحب ريا يُرى ، ولكن حاجتهم بأن لا يحب ربا يأفل" ؛ إذ هو دليل عدم الدوام، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, the argument of Ibrahim with his people concerning the stars, and what was mentioned of setting and disappearance. And he did not argue with them that He does not love a Lord who is seen, but he argued with them that He does not love a Lord who sets; for that is a proof of lack of permanence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage provides an additional proof from the story of Prophet Ibrahim. Maturidi notes that Ibrahim argued against his people's worship of celestial bodies by pointing out that they set (afala), not by saying that they cannot be seen. Ibrahim's argument was against setting, not visibility. This implies that if a being is permanent and does not set, like Allah, then being seen is not a problem. The point is that Ibrahim did not use the inability to see as an argument against divinity, but used the impermanence of setting. This supports the possibility of seeing Allah, since Allah is everlasting and does not set.

Key Terms
إبراهيم
أفول
غيبة
دوام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Ibrahim argued against the possibility of seeing a deity because he said he does not love a Lord who is seen.
Correction: In fact, Ibrahim argued against a Lord who sets, not one who is seen. This shows that visibility is not a flaw in divinity; impermanence is.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi consider Ibrahim's argument as proof for the possibility of seeing Allah?
A) Because Ibrahim argued that a Lord who is seen cannot be divine.
B) Because Ibrahim argued against worshipping celestial bodies because they set, not because they are unseen, so visibility is not a flaw.
C) Because Ibrahim saw the stars.
D) Because the stars are seen.

Key Takeaway
Ibrahim's argument against setting, not visibility, supports the possibility of seeing Allah because Allah is permanent and never sets.

?

Moses' Request as Proof for the Possibility of Seeing Allah
Maturidi argues that Moses' request proves vision is possible and refutes the sign interpretation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the request of Prophet Moses (peace be upon him) to establish the rational possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife.

Core Idea
A prophet would not ask for the impossible – Moses' request proves vision is possible.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument from the Qur'an: the request of Prophet Moses to see Allah. If seeing Allah were impossible, Moses – a prophet – would not have asked for it. Furthermore, Allah's response, which conditions vision upon an event, implies that vision is possible under certain conditions. This lesson unpacks that argument and addresses an important objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then carefully read the literal translation. Let the argument unfold in its original sequence.
Notice the objection and the three numbered refutations.
2
Identify the main proof
Maturidi's primary proof is the fact that Moses asked to see Allah. Ask yourself: why would a prophet ask for something impossible?
This is the core premise: a prophet cannot be ignorant of his Lord.
3
Understand the objection
An opponent might say that Moses was actually asking for a sign, not for vision. Notice how Maturidi acknowledges this objection before refuting it.
4
Examine the three refutations
Maturidi gives three reasons why the sign interpretation fails. Read each one carefully and see how they build on the text.
First: 'You will not see Me' but He showed him something. Second: asking for signs is obstinacy. Third: the condition with the mountain implies vision.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument establishes the possibility (jawāz) of seeing Allah. Why is this important for theology?
It counters those who deny the vision of Allah in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني : قول موسى عليه السلام : رَبِّ أَرِنِي أَنْظُرُ إِلَيْكَ، ولو كان لا يجوز الرؤية لكان ذلك السؤال منه جهل بربه ، ومن يجهله لا يحتمل أن يكون موضعا لرسالته أمينا على وحيه . وبعاد ، فإن الله تعالى لم ينهه ولا أيأسه ، وبدون ذلك نهى نوحا وعاتب آدم وغيرهما من الرسل ، وذلك لو كان لا يجوز يبلغ الكفر . ثم قال : فإِنْ اسْتَقَرَّ مَكَانَهُ فَسَوْفَ تَرَانِي ، فإن قيل : لعله سأل آية يعلم فإن قيل : لعله سأل آية يعلم بها ، قيل : لا يحتمل ذا ارجوه : أحدها أنه قال : لَنْ تَرَانِي " ، وقد أراه " . وأيضا إن طلب الآيات يخرج مخرج التعنت ، أو قد أراه الآيات ، وذلك تعنت الكثرة ، انهم لا يزالون يطلبون الآيات وإن كانت الكفاية قد ثبتت فمثله ذلك . وأيضا أنه قال : " فإن استقر مكانه فسوف ترانى، ، والآية التي يستقر معها الجبل دون الآية التي لا يستقر معها . ثبت أنه لم يرد بذلك الآية ، ولا قوة إلا بالله
And the second: the statement of Moses, peace be upon him: 'My Lord, show me [Yourself] that I may look at You.' And if vision were not permissible, that request would be ignorance of his Lord on his part, and whoever is ignorant of Him cannot possibly be a place for His message and trustworthy over His revelation. And further, Allah Almighty did not forbid him nor make him despair, whereas without that He forbade Noah and rebuked Adam and other messengers. And that, if it were not permissible, would reach disbelief. Then He said: 'But if it settles in its place, then you will see Me.' If it is said: Perhaps he asked for a sign by which he would know, it is said: This is not plausible in three [reasons]: one, that He said 'You will not see Me,' and yet He showed him. Also, asking for signs comes out as obstinacy, and He had already shown him signs, and that is the obstinacy of the many: they keep asking for signs even though sufficiency has been established, so likewise that. Also, He said: 'But if it settles in its place, then you will see Me,' and the sign by which the mountain settles is different from the sign by which it does not settle. It is established that he did not intend by that a sign. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of seeing Allah in the afterlife, based on the request of Moses (peace be upon him). The author begins by stating that if seeing Allah were impossible, Moses' request to see Him would be ignorant, but Moses was a prophet, so it cannot be ignorance. Furthermore, Allah did not forbid him or give him despair, whereas He did forbid and rebuke other prophets for lesser matters. If it were impossible, that would lead to disbelief. Then the verse 'If it settles in its place, then you will see Me' is quoted. An objection is raised: perhaps Moses asked for a sign. The author responds that this is implausible for three reasons: 1) Allah initially said 'You will not see Me' but then showed him something; 2) Seeking signs is often an act of obstinacy, and Moses had already seen signs; 3) The condition with the mountain settling indicates that seeing is conditional, not that it is a sign. Thus, the request was for actual vision, not a sign. The author concludes that this argument shows the possibility of seeing Allah.

Key Terms
الرؤية
موسى عليه السلام
رؤية الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses asked for a sign (āyah) from Allah, not actual vision.
Correction: Maturidi shows through three reasons that the request was for actual vision: (1) Allah had already shown signs, (2) asking for signs is obstinacy, (3) the condition relates to vision not signs.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why does Moses' request prove the possibility of seeing Allah?
A) Because Moses was a prophet and knew Allah cannot be seen.
B) Because a prophet would not ask for what is impossible, and Allah's response implies possibility.
C) Because the mountain settled, showing vision is possible.
D) Because Allah did not forbid Moses outright.

Key Takeaway
The request of Moses establishes that seeing Allah is possible, as no prophet would ask for the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Moses, a prophet of immense station, yearned so deeply to see Allah that he made an audacious request. Do you feel any comparable longing for Allah in your heart? What does your daily preoccupation reveal about your true desire for the vision of your Lord?
?
If a prophet's request for vision was not rebuked, why do you live as if seeing Allah is distant or irrelevant to your life today? How would your actions change if you truly believed that you will one day see Him, and that your current choices prepare you for that moment?
?
Maturidi argues that a prophet would never ask for what is impossible. Are there things you consider impossible in your relationship with Allah, such as forgiveness, closeness, or change? What would it mean for you to ask Allah for what seems impossible, trusting that He can grant it?

Abraham's Argument on Stars: Visibility vs. Permanence
Maturidi uses Abraham's argument to show that visibility is not a flaw.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Abraham's argument about the setting of celestial bodies refutes the denial of the possibility of seeing Allah, by distinguishing between impermanence and visibility.

Core Idea
Abraham argued against impermanence, not visibility, so seeing Allah is possible.

Imam Maturidi presents a subtle proof for the possibility of seeing Allah by analyzing Prophet Abraham's debate with his people about celestial bodies. Abraham did not reject worship of something that can be seen; rather, he rejected worship of something that sets and disappears. This shows that visibility is not a flaw—only impermanence is. Understanding this distinction is key to refuting those who deny seeing Allah in the hereafter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key phrases: 'لم يحاجهم بأن لا يحب ربا يُرى' (he did not argue that he does not love a lord that is seen) and 'ولكن حاجتهم بأن لا يحب ربا يأفل' (but he argued that he does not love a lord that sets).
The phrase 'ريا' is a typo for 'ربا'.
2
Understand the argument's structure
Abraham makes a distinction: he does not reject worship of something just because it is seen. He rejects worship of something that sets—i.e., that is temporary. The setting of celestial bodies is a proof of their impermanence (عدم الدوام).
This distinction is the core of Maturidi's proof for the possibility of ru'ya.
3
Connect to the theological context
The denial of seeing Allah is often based on the idea that Allah is invisible like physical objects. But Abraham did not use invisibility as a flaw; he used impermanence. Therefore, if Allah is eternal and permanent, visibility is not a disqualifying attribute.
This supports the Sunni position that Allah can be seen in the hereafter.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some may think Abraham argued that a lord cannot be seen. Actually, he argued that a lord must be permanent. This corrects the misunderstanding that visibility equals unworthiness.
The common misunderstanding is addressed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا محاجة ابراهيم قومه في النجوم ، وما ذكر بالأفول والغيبة ، ولم يحاجهم بأن لا يحب ريا يُرى ، ولكن حاجتهم بأن لا يحب ربا يأفل" ؛ إذ هو دليل عدم الدوام، ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also the argument of Abraham, peace be upon him, with his people regarding the stars, and what he mentioned about setting and disappearance. He did not argue against them by [saying] that he does not love a lord that is seen, but rather he argued against them by [saying] that he does not love a lord that sets, for that is a proof of impermanence. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of seeing Allah. The author cites Abraham's argument with his people. Abraham argued by pointing out that the celestial bodies set and disappear, which indicates they are not worthy of lordship. However, he did not argue that they cannot be seen; rather, he argued about their temporality. This implies that visibility itself is not a flaw. Thus, denying vision of Allah based on the argument that He is not like physical objects that can be seen is invalid. The focus is on the attribute of permanence, not visibility.

Key Terms
محاجة ابراهيم
الأفول
الغيبة
الرب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Abraham argued that a lord cannot be seen.
Correction: Abraham argued against impermanence (setting), not visibility.

Checkpoint
What point did Abraham make against his people's worship of celestial bodies?
A) That they cannot be seen.
B) That they set and disappear, proving they are not eternal.
C) That they are too far away.
D) That they are made of fire.

Key Takeaway
Abraham's argument shows that impermanence is the flaw in worship, not visibility, thus opening the door for the possibility of seeing Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the possibility of seeing Allah, do you instinctively feel it is impossible because He is 'too spiritual'? How does Abraham's distinction between visibility and impermanence challenge that assumption? What would change in your worship if you truly believed you could see Him?
?

?
Have you ever rejected a truth about Allah because it seemed too 'physical' or 'worldly' (like seeing Him)? How can Abraham's example of not dismissing visibility help you open your heart to the full reality of who Allah is? What comfort does this give you?

Four Arguments Against the Mu'tazila Interpretation of 'Looking'
Maturidi enumerates four arguments against the Mu'tazila interpretation of 'nazira' as waiting.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's four linguistic and contextual arguments that refute the Mu'tazila claim that 'nazira' in the verse means 'waiting' rather than literal vision.

Core Idea
The Mu'tazila interpretation of 'nazira' contradicts the Qur'anic context and Arabic usage.

The Mu'tazila argued that the verse "وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة" does not imply actual vision of Allah but rather a waiting for reward. Imam Maturidi presents four compelling arguments from the verse itself and the nature of the Hereafter to refute this interpretation. These arguments demonstrate that the literal meaning is the only coherent reading.

Guided Path
1
Locate the four arguments in the text
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the four numbered reasons that Maturidi gives against the interpretation of 'nazira' as waiting.
The passage explicitly lists them as 'أحدها', 'والثانى', 'والثالث', 'والرابع'.
2
Understand the first argument
Maturidi argues that the Hereafter is not a place of waiting; waiting pertains to this world. The verse describes a state of the Hereafter, so waiting is inappropriate.
3
Analyze the second and third arguments
The second argument notes that 'nādira' (radiant) indicates reward already occurring. The third argument uses the preposition 'ila' to show that the looking is actual, not metaphorical.
The preposition 'ila' in Arabic is used for physical direction, not waiting.
4
Reflect on the fourth argument
The verse is a glad tiding of blessings, not a statement of waiting. Interpreting it as waiting would distort the intended meaning.
This argument highlights the purpose of the verse as good news.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا قوله تعالى : وُجُوهٌ يَوْمَئِذٍ نَاضِرَةٌ ، إِلَى رَبِّهَا نَاظِرَةٌ ، ثم لا يحتمل مع ذلك الانتظار لأوجه : أحدها أن الآخرة ليست لوقت الانتظار ، إنما هي الدنيا ، هي دار الوقوع والوجود ، إلا وقت الفزع ، وقيل : إنهم يعاينوا في أنفسهم ما له حق الوقوع . والثانى قوله : وجوه يومئذ ناضرة، وذلك وقوع الثواب . والثالث قوله : إلى ربها ناظرة ، وإلى حرف يستعمل فى النظر إلى الشيء لا في الانتظار . والرابع أن القول به يخرج مخرج البشارة تعظيم ما نالوا من النعم ، والانتظار ما كان الصرف عن حقيقة المفهوم قضاءً على الله ، فيلزم القول ليس منه ، بالنظر إلى الله كما قال ، على نفى جميع معانى الشبه عن الله سبحانه ، مثل ما أضيف إليه من الكلام والفعل والقدرة والإرادة ، يجب الوصف به معانى الشبه ، وكذلك القول بالهستية . فمن زعم أن الله تعالى لا يقدر أن يكرم أحدا بالرؤية فهو يقدر بالرؤية التي فهمها من الخلق ، وإن كان القول بالرحمن على العرش استوى ، وغير ذلك من الآيات لا يجب دفعها بالعرض على المفهوم من الخلق ، بل يحقق ذلك على نفى الشبه ، فمثله خبر الرؤية على نفى جميع معانى الشبه . والله الموفق .
And also His statement, Exalted is He: 'Faces that Day are radiant, looking at their Lord.' Then [the interpretation of] 'waiting' (intizar) is not plausible for that, for aspects: First, that the Hereafter is not a time of waiting; rather the world is the abode of occurrence and existence, except the time of terror. And it is said that they witness in themselves what has the right to occur. Second, His statement: 'Faces that Day are radiant' - that is the occurrence of reward. Third, His statement: 'to their Lord looking' - and the preposition 'ila' is used for looking at something, not for waiting. Fourth, that the statement [of vision] comes as glad tidings magnifying what they have attained of blessings, whereas 'waiting' would be a turning away from the true meaning, a judgment upon Allah, so it would follow that the statement [of vision] is not from Him, regarding looking at Allah as He said, upon negating all meanings of likeness (tashbih) from Allah, Glorified is He, like what is attributed to Him of speech, action, power, and will; one must describe with [negating] meanings of likeness, and likewise the statement of 'essence' (hastiyya?). So whoever claims that Allah Almighty is not able to honor anyone with vision, then he is [merely] measuring according to the vision he understood from creation, even if the statement 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne' and other verses must not be rejected by comparing them to what is understood from creation; rather, that is affirmed upon negating likeness. So likewise the report of vision is upon negating all meanings of likeness. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 677

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the Mu'tazila interpretation of the verse 'وجوه يومئذ ناضرة إلى ربها ناظرة'. The Mu'tazila claimed that 'ناظرة' here means 'waiting' (intizar) for reward, not literal vision. The author gives four arguments against this: (1) The Hereafter is not a place of waiting; waiting is for this world. (2) The phrase 'radiant' indicates reward already occurring. (3) The preposition 'to' (إلى) in Arabic is used for actual looking, not waiting. (4) The verse is a glad tiding, not a promise of waiting. Then the author discusses the principle of negating likeness (tashbih) when describing Allah: we affirm the attribute (vision) while negating any similarity to creation. The Mu'tazila who deny vision are guilty of measuring Allah by created standards. Thus, the correct approach is to affirm the literal meaning while negating any anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
ناظرة
الانتظار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that 'nazira' means waiting for reward, not actual vision of Allah.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the context, language, and purpose of the verse all point to literal vision, not waiting.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's third argument, the preposition 'ila' indicates that 'nazira' means:
A) waiting
B) looking at something
C) hoping
D) expecting

Key Takeaway
The four linguistic and contextual arguments demonstrate that the verse clearly affirms the believers' vision of Allah in the Hereafter, not mere waiting.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a clear verse of the Qur'an, do you submit to its apparent meaning even if it challenges your preconceived notions? What prevents you from doing so?
?
The Mu'tazila twisted this verse to fit their philosophy. Are there areas in your life where you rationalize away clear commands of Allah to follow your own desires? What would it cost you to obey fully?
?
If you truly believe you will see Allah in Paradise, how does that certainty change the way you live today? Are you preparing for that ultimate encounter, or are you distracted by the temporary?

Affirming Vision While Negating Likeness (Tashbih)
Maturidi explains the methodology of affirming divine attributes while negating likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that when affirming Allah's attributes, we must negate all resemblance to creation, and how this applies to the vision of Allah.

Core Idea
We affirm the vision of Allah literally, but without any likeness to human vision.

After refuting the Mu'tazila interpretation of 'nazira' as waiting, Maturidi addresses the broader issue of how to understand divine attributes. He emphasizes that we must affirm the literal meaning of the verse while firmly negating any similarity (tashbih) between Allah and His creation. This principle applies to all attributes, such as speech, power, and will.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on negating tashbih
Read the Arabic text starting from 'فيلزم القول ليس منه' onward. Notice how Maturidi connects the previous arguments to the principle of negating likeness.
This section is more methodological than the previous one.
2
Understand the principle of negating likeness
Maturidi states that when we say 'Allah sees', we must negate any similarity to created vision. This is how we affirm all attributes revealed in the Qur'an and Sunnah.
3
See the application to vision and other attributes
Maturidi gives examples: the throne verse and other verses must be affirmed while negating tashbih. The same applies to the vision of Allah.
The error of the Mu'tazila is that they deny the attribute because they mistakenly think affirming it requires anthropomorphism.

The Argument from 'Al-Husna wa Ziyada' for the Vision of Allah
Maturidi uses the hadith interpretation of 'ziyada' as vision to argue for its possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the interpretation of 'ziyada' as vision to argue that seeing Allah is a possible apparent meaning in the verse.

Core Idea
If vision were impossible, the hadith wouldn't interpret 'more' as vision.

Maturidi presents a logical argument rooted in the verse 'For those who do good is the best and more.' He notes that a hadith interprets 'more' as looking at Allah. While other interpretations exist, the mere fact that this interpretation is accepted shows that the concept of vision must already be an apparent (zahir) meaning in the verse. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and hadith reference
Start by reading the Arabic verse and its translation. Then notice the hadith that interprets 'ziyada' as vision. This is the key evidence.
The verse is Qur'an 10:26.
2
Understand the logical inference
Maturidi argues: if the concept of seeing Allah were not an apparent meaning in the verse, then the hadith would not be able to divert the apparent meaning of the verse to that interpretation. Follow the logic carefully.
This is a deductive argument about the nature of interpretation.
3
Connect to the broader doctrine
The conclusion that vision is an apparent meaning supports the Sunni doctrine that seeing Allah is possible (for the believers in the afterlife). Reflect on how this fits into the larger discussion of divine attributes.
This argument is a key proof in kalam for the possibility of ru'yat Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا قوله تعالى : لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ، وجاء في غير خبر النظر إلى الله ، وقد يحتمل غير ذلك مما جاء فيه التفسير ، لكنه لولا أن القول بالرؤية كان أمرا ظاهرا لم يحتمل صرف ظاهر - لم يجىء فيها – إليها ويدفع به الخبر ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also His statement, Exalted is He: 'For those who do good is the best (al-husna) and more (ziyada).' And it has come in other reports [that the 'more' is] looking at Allah. And that could be understood other than that from what has come of interpretation. However, were it not that the statement of vision was an apparent (thing), it would not be possible to divert an apparent (meaning) - which does not occur in it - to it, and to push away the report. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the possibility of seeing Allah based on the verse 'للذين أحسنوا الحسنى وزيادة' (For those who do good is the best and more). The author notes that there is a hadith that interprets 'more' (ziyada) as looking at Allah. Although other interpretations exist, the author argues that if the concept of vision were not an apparent (zahir) meaning, the hadith would not be used to interpret the verse in that way. Thus, the fact that the hadith is accepted as an interpretation shows that the possibility of vision is already implied in the verse. This reinforces the doctrine of seeing Allah.

Key Terms
الحسنى
الزيادة
النظر إلى الله
الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'more' in the verse refers to something other than vision, such as extra reward.
Correction: While other interpretations exist, the hadith's interpretation of 'more' as vision shows that vision is a valid apparent meaning of the verse, otherwise the hadith would not be used to interpret it that way.

Checkpoint
What is the argument from the verse 'للذين أحسنوا الحسنى وزيادة' regarding vision?
A) That 'more' means paradise.
B) That the hadith interpreting 'more' as vision would be invalid if vision were not possible.
C) That the verse explicitly states vision.
D) That the verse is abrogated.

Key Takeaway
The hadith's interpretation of 'ziyada' as vision demonstrates that vision is an apparent (zahir) meaning in the verse.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the 'more' in the verse is interpreted as looking at Allah, does your heart long for that vision, or are you distracted by the pleasures of this world? Commit to renewing your desire for the ultimate reward.
?
If the concept of seeing Allah is so important that a hadith interprets a Qur'anic verse to affirm it, how does that affect your daily actions? Do you live in a way that seeks al-husna wa ziyada?
?
Maturidi uses a logical argument to defend the possibility of vision. Do you rely on both textual and rational proofs in your faith? Are there aspects of your belief that you have not examined rationally? Resolve to strengthen your understanding with reason and scripture.

5 min
The Verse 'al-ḥusnā wa ziyāda' and the Vision of Allah
Maturidi argues that the clear report of vision overrides speculative interpretation of the verse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the Qur'anic verse 'al-ḥusnā wa ziyāda' and a prophetic report to argue for the permissibility of seeing Allah in the Hereafter.

Core Idea
Clear reports override speculative interpretation of verses.

Imam Maturidi presents an additional argument for the vision of Allah using the verse 'For those who do good is the best and more.' He notes that a report explains 'more' as the vision of Allah, and argues that since the doctrine of vision is well-established, one cannot dismiss the clear report by reinterpreting the verse. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the literal translation. Let Maturidi's own words come through.
Notice the phrase 'لم يجىء فيها' which may be a gloss.
2
Identify the elements
Identify the verse (al-ḥusnā wa ziyāda), the report (khabar), and Maturidi's reasoning. What is the 'apparent matter' he refers to?
The 'apparent matter' is the doctrine of vision being well-known.
3
Understand the argument
Maturidi argues that because the doctrine of vision is apparent (zāhir), it is not permissible to turn away from the clear meaning of the report by interpreting the verse differently.
This is a principle of tafsir: a clear text (khabar) takes precedence over speculative interpretation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا قوله تعالى : لِلَّذِينَ أَحْسَنُوا الحُسْنَى وَزِيَادَةٌ، وجاء في غير خبر النظر إلى الله ، وقد يحتمل غير ذلك مما جاء فيه التفسير ، لكنه لولا أن القول بالرؤية كان أمرا ظاهرا لم يحتمل صرف ظاهر – لم يجىء فيها – إليها ويدفع به الخبر. ولا قوة إلا بالله
And also His saying, Exalted is He: 'For those who do good is the best (al-ḥusnā) and more (ziyāda)' (10:26). And it has come in a report other than the vision of Allah. And it may admit other [interpretations] as have come in commentary. However, were it not that the doctrine of vision was an apparent matter, it would not be possible to turn away the apparent meaning – it did not come in it – to it and thereby reject the [explicit] report. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 678

Commentary
This passage is an additional argument. Maturidi cites the verse 'For those who do good is the best (al-ḥusnā) and more (ziyāda)'. He notes that a hadith interprets 'more' as the vision of Allah. While the verse might admit other interpretations, the key point is that since the doctrine of vision is well-established and clear, it is not permissible to set aside the clear report (the hadith) by resorting to speculative interpretation. The phrase 'لم يجىء فيها' possibly indicates that the apparent meaning of the verse does not explicitly mention vision, but the hadith clarifies it.

?
Maturidi argues that a clear report should not be set aside by speculation. Are there any teachings of Islam that you have quietly dismissed because they seemed 'unlikely' to your modern mind? Will you commit to accepting them as true, even if you cannot fully grasp them?
